### Post by fr4nko on 2009-05-19
Hi all,

I would like to share my experience about the different IDEs on linux.

Time over time I've tried several IDEs but I always came back to the old good emacs. I believe that emacs is excellent for C/C++ programming used with gdb for debugging. You just have to be patient at the beginning and learn how to use it - emacs is not a stupid point and click applications, you have to be patient and take the time to learn it. The good thing is that, once you know the most important commands and key shortcuts, you will be soon very productive and you will appreciate the power of emacs.
Among the features that makes emacs so useful (for me):

[LIST]
[*]the macro utilities is something very useful that enable you to automatize small repetitive tasks (the key bindings are "CTRL-X (", "CTRL-x )" and "CTRL-x e" respectively for starting, ending macro recording and apply the recorded macro). You can also use "CTRL-U <n>" to repeat the macro n times
[*]the support for regular expression is great and gives you a lot of power (the basic functions are replace-regexp and search-regexp).
[/LIST]
 
I think also the the "info" browser integrated in emacs is great, by using "CTRL-h i" I've access to the full libc/gcc documentation with an easy navigation system. Something which is also very useful is that you can click on compilation error messages and emacs brings you to the incriminated point of the source code.

Something I'm not using but I think could be very useful is etags in order to scan your C files and easily locate function declarations and implementation. I believe that if you are beginning to use emacs it is something worth a try.

As a more mundane detail, it is useful to know that now you can have smooth antialiased fonts with emacs. You have to grab emacs-snapshot and modify some configurations files (there are several articles on the net and I've made also a post on that: "                **emacs with nice anti aliased smooth fonts"**)

In my experience commercial IDE of full featured IDE are often bloated and/or instable and I prefer to avoid them. For example I've tried Komodo IDE and Komodo Edit, I think both of them are very interesting but there are some reasons that prevented me from adopting any of them:

[LIST]
[*]Komodo IDE is not freely available (and it is not free software)
[*]Komodo Edit is freely available but is not free software
[*]very long to startup / big memory footprint and I've observed some instabilities
[/LIST]
 The ***positive points*** of Komodo are, in my point of view:

[LIST]
[*] very good syntax highlighting and automatic indentation for many different languages
[*]excellent system for code completions and hints about function usage (this is especially useful for Python/PHP)
[/LIST]
 
Having said that, Komodo is quite good and probably it is worth a try. I'm using it for PHP code but for everything else I'm still using the old good emacs.

For **python** I believe tha **IDLE** itself is not bad especially now, because with jaunty jackalope you can have nice antialised fonts (see my post on emacs with antialiased fonts for that).

I hope this post will be useful for you. Enjoy ubuntu, enjoy linux! :D

Francesco

### Post by Ariel David Moya Sequeira on 2009-06-11
Most of the following are simple editors with syntax highlighting, but they are my preferred programming tools:

[LIST]
[*]C++, JAVA & family: **NetBeans**
[*]LaTeX, BibTex, Lilypond & family: **Kile**
[*]Assembly: **Kate** (nothing better than simplicity)
[*]Any LOGO dialect: **itself**. Almost of them are their own IDE.
[*]Scheme: **PLT Scheme**.
[*]Pascal, C, Python and others: **Geany**
[/LIST]
Unfortunately, I haven't found the best-for-all IDE. All editors have pros and cons :(.

### Post by schmendrick on 2009-07-29
c# / c++ (win):
visual studio express. 2 main reasons: 
fast responsiveness, best debugger

c# (linux):
monodevelop: kudos to those guys. really a great IDE, also given for the fact that its a quite small team (in comparison to netbeans and eclipse team). unforntunately c++ support is still crap.

c++(linux/gcc):

i installed the new qt creator a week ago (allthough i do not create QT apps) and i highly recommend it. the name suggests its for QT developers, but every other developer should give it a look as well. 
its small, its fast, and it has a nearly as good debugging support as VisualC debugger provides. plus i can create my own helpfiles for it out of doxygen documentation and directly integrate it in the editor. 
i have to say i am astonished! thats the best IDE ive found so far.

i tried till now all big IDEs (netbeans, kdevelop, anjuta, codeblocks) for quite some time, and until recently i then sellted with eclipse.
because of its formidable syntax-highlighting and code-parsing, its on-mouse-hover-infos ,and its ok-debugger support. 
but i pretty much disliked the rest of it. i find it slow, unintuitive, very undocumented features and no good community support (take some hours to check the bugzilla mailing list of eclipse and its CDT project to see what i mean).

but thankfully i now have qt creator.

java:
netbeans

shellscripts/python/PHP:
gedit

### Post by schmendrick on 2010-01-21
sorry, for the late reply i didnt read the thread any more up until now


> **fr4nko said:**
> I'm an old school emacs user but I was willing to try Qt Creator because you said it is so interesting. I was surprised at the beginning: no way to create a C or C++ project ? I mean, a project not using Qt...

Francesco

very easy to do so, 95% of the time i use qt creator while not-creating qt-projects.

but maybe the names are not that intuitive:

file -> new file or project -> 
now either 
* take "Import of makefile-based project" and choose a destination to use. he will automatically call "make" inside that project there, so you would have to create your own Makefile inside that folder.

or 

*  take "Empty QT4 project", add a file "main.cpp" and build. you can build normal apps by that as well. 
open your .pro file and add: 
> TEMPLATE = app
CONFIG -= qt
to the code. Now when making the project he shouldnt link against qt-libraries any more.

---

### Post by duckfeet on 2010-03-22
For beginners, who really want to learn how things work, I'm glad I was steered away from IDE's--as the professor had his favorite--and towards just using a text editor...I found that once I got used to VIM, I could hop around fairly easy in java.  I'm now interested in Android development, and see the same thing happening with Eclipse being recommended, but again, I understand things better, and can work quick enough with VIM, so I think I'll avoid IDE's for the near future anyway...I got used to VI a long time ago, on a unix workstation, so perhaps that is why I find VIM so accessible...but once you go thru the vimtutor, and get the basic hang of it--a couple hours, maybe--you can add on the rest, as you need it...and very easy to stay in 'command line' mode, rather than hop back and forth between windows and command line on ubuntu...

### Post by J3Rik0 on 2010-04-17
First of all "HELLO WORLD!" :)) i've just made a hello world program just by greeting all you programmers out there...i'm not yet a full fleged programmer, i'm a designer..new to linux (well not so new i'm aspiring to become an IT Guru *dreams* so i have tried a hell lot of software and os's but i find linux to my liking) anyway here are my opinions on the IDE subject.

I personally use a text editor because i hate auto complete (although productive it may be when i'm at work, in my free time TEXT EDITOR ALL THE WAY) you never learn a god damn thing when the program already knows what you are trying to do (witch for me is irritating) i'm talking from a beginers perspective.

Cutting it short cuz i like to brag alot here are my suggestions (nubs and pros)...

TE = text editor; IDE = i wonder

Windows: Notepad++(TE) will take the first prize (syntax highlight for anything from A to Z, quite a lot of plug-ins to and growing), E-Text Editor (TE) - textmate for windows although it still needs some polish (still in development) but i'm starting to love it, Aptana Studio(IDE) - i found it a short time ago and liking it.

Linux: default Gedit (being a TE lover), same Aptana Studio, but i'm starting to get used to Scribes (already found a bug) but i'm stiking to gedit until a big part of the bugs are taken care of. 

OSX: didn't have time to test IDE's on it but TextMate is doing wonders for me

Any questions/suggestions/advices on editor's, IDE's and other development software (any kind of software for that matter) PM me ;) i will be glad to help out.

### Post by Some Penguin on 2011-02-07
After having using IntelliJ for some weeks (after Eclipse), I'd have to say that IntelliJ feels a lot less like repeatedly punching myself in the face by using tools that appear to be written by complete incompetents.  Eclipse has *major* issues with Maven integration, and with stability and performance in general -- there is little excuse for an IDE to randomly die with a segmentation fault before it even puts up a single window, for instance, or for a right-click context menu to occasionally take minutes to appear on a very performant Mac Pro.  

IntelliJ doesn't get in my way or waste my time *nearly* as much.

### Post by psusi on 2011-03-02

That doesn't make sense.  DDD is a graphical interface that runs gdb.  So is emacs ( gud mode ).  You run gdb under one, or the other, but not both.

### Post by psusi on 2011-03-22
> **muteXe said:**
> Sorry for the naive question, but how on earth do you debug your code if you're using something like vim, emacs or gedit?

Cheers,

mute

With vim or gedit, you are on your own; you have to run gdb yourself and that is quite limiting.  When you run it under emacs, you open the full source files like you normally edit them, and you can click on a line to set a breakpoint, and single step through.  You can also do things like open a separate watch window and so forth.

You can also compile your program under emacs and it opens another window and shows you the results where you can hit enter on an error and the other window will jump to the offending line of code.

---

### Post by Simian Man on 2011-03-22
> **andrew1992 said:**
> Our brains are the best debugger. Clear thinking and logic beats any debugger provided by an IDE.

Have you ever worked on a project with more than 100K SLOC?  With projects of that scale, I don't care how big your brain is, just staring at the code isn't going to help any.

---

### Post by andrew1992 on 2011-03-22
It doesn't matter how large the project is. When debugging, you're dealing with a small subsection of the code. If you can take a minute to think clearly about what went wrong, it's quite easy to localize the error.

---

### Post by Simian Man on 2011-03-22
> **andrew1992 said:**
> It doesn't matter how large the project is. When debugging, you're dealing with a small subsection of the code. If you can take a minute to think clearly about what went wrong, it's quite easy to localize the error.

You didn't answer the question, have you really worked with any large projects?

Even for really well written code bases, what you say isn't true.  Oftentimes two or more sections of code will look correct in and of themselves, but will make assumptions that violate each other.  For example if one function calls another, but passes an argument that the other function does not expect, you will have a hard to debug problem.

And, I hate to break it to you, but most large projects are NOT well written.  Debugging large, messy code bases where almost all of the code was written by others is an onerous task.  Trying to do so without a debugger is a nightmare.

---

### Post by andrew1992 on 2011-03-22
If by large you mean 100k, then no. 5k where I wrote only one method in the entire project, yes. With debugging, that's not relevant. The situation you just described, as I previously pointed out, strips the entire code base down to two functions, and it's just a logic error. With clear thinking this is easy to localize, and I've had plenty of experience doing so with code I'm not familiar with.

---

### Post by Simian Man on 2011-03-22
> **andrew1992 said:**
> If by large you mean 100k, then no. 5k where I wrote only one method in the entire project, yes. With debugging, that's not relevant. The situation you just described, as I previously pointed out, strips the entire code base down to two functions, and it's just a logic error. With clear thinking this is easy to localize, and I've had plenty of experience doing so with code I'm not familiar with.

Well, 5K is still a pretty small project.  And in the situation I described, it's not necessarily limited to two functions.  I just described those two because, by looking at them alone, you would never see a problem.  The second function could pass the bad data to other functions, or store it in a data structure, or do anything else that could cause problems further down the road without even giving a hint of what the original problem was.

The point is that, with a suitably large project, it's impossible to fit the entire code base into your mind at once.  Also, with a program, no matter how well written, it's still probable that errors will develop that can't be seen by just eye-balling the code.

I work on compilers and have spent *days* debugging single errors.  When doing optimizations, it's extremely hard to track down when errors are introduced into the code - and it's usually down to incorrect analysis which then must be tracked down itself.  For some things, you're right, a debugger isn't necessary.  But for debugging tasks I do, it is absolutely an essential tool.

---

### Post by andrew1992 on 2011-03-22
I guess I'll leave it to later projects for me to discover whether or not an IDE debugger can advantageous at times. My opinion holds that one shouldn't ever have to fit an entire codebase into their mind after having properly thought about where the error occurred (not just eye-balling it). However, I do agree that I can't speak for all situations without working on very large projects.  The point is, debugging when writing code using vim/gedit is certainly feasible, and at least for me always preferable. :)

### Post by Some Penguin on 2011-03-23
> **andrew1992 said:**
> I guess I'll leave it to later projects for me to discover whether or not an IDE debugger can advantageous at times. My opinion holds that one shouldn't ever have to fit an entire codebase into their mind after having properly thought about where the error occurred (not just eye-balling it). However, I do agree that I can't speak for all situations without working on very large projects.  The point is, debugging when writing code using vim/gedit is certainly feasible, and at least for me always preferable. :)

Errors can be quite subtle.  In real-world situations where you're not writing a project completely from scratch in order to convince a professor that you understand something, errors don't even have to be in code written by anybody in your entire organization.  This makes it even more interesting finding subtle errors because nobody was is necessarily familiar with the particular design decisions made by the original authors, and whether seemingly odd code is cleverly resolving some edge case you haven't yet noticed, or whether it's completely wrong and the cause of all your miseries.

This is particularly fun when you have highly concurrent systems.  Systems that live-lock but not predictably and very seldom (and thus highly likely to be caused by fairly rare race conditions); extremely slow memory leaks; finding exactly which piece of code is not MT-safe; finding out which piece of third-party code fails to live up to its alleged API; identifying why individual queries are slow while functionally identical queries are fast -- these are not unreal tasks for which profilers and debuggers are rather useful.  These are also what cost time.  It's relatively simple to find out that somebody's screwed up by dereferencing null because that tends to cause things to explode immediately; finding incorrectly handled edge cases in complex algorithms or anything involving extreme concurrency is often not simple.

---

### Post by matthew.ball on 2011-03-24
> **muteXe said:**
> Cheers mate, never really looked at emacs, always edited files with vim.  I will be looking into emacs + gdb.

Tom
If you're interested in using emacs (which is a good idea in my opinion), I can help you out.

Vanilla emacs doesn't appear all that good (unfortunately it doesn't ship with a customisation file, and that's where all the bells-and-whistles are activated), and unless you spend some time going through the in-built tutorial, or just generally finding a customisation tutorial, you probably won't discover what makes emacs *really* good.

I would strongly recommend using the [emacs-gtk]("http://dev.pocoo.org/~gbrandl/emacs2.png") (which I believe is the default emacs install these days) instead of the [emacs-nox]("http://screenshots.debian.net/screenshots/e/emacs23-nox/5881_large.png") install. Of course, that image of emacs-gtk has some customisations turned on (mainly just the ["Collection of Emacs Development Environment Tools"]("http://cedet.sourceforge.net/")), which is not too had to configure.

Anyway, if you don't want to go to the effort of finding the perfect customisations for you, I would suggest checking out the [emacs-starter-kit]("http://eschulte.github.com/emacs-starter-kit/") which is basically a collection of emacs lisp code designed to bring the absolute power out of emacs (and on top of this, you can customise it further!), it's really useful (though I haven't used it, but I have decided to promote it, if I am going to recommend emacs - most people probably aren't interested in spending hours finding customisations online).

There's also a [wiki]("http://www.emacswiki.org/") which has documentation on most things (of course, not everything), and is just very useful to search through if you're having difficulties with something.

---

### Post by matthew.ball on 2011-03-25
> **might and power said:**
> is lisp essential to the functionality (extreme customisation) of something like emacs? i was thinking i might right a minimal emacs clone for .Net / mono
Emacs *is* a lisp environment first-and-foremost.

As psusi has aid, there's a tiny bit of C in emacs - to provide the lisp environment. While the rest of emacs is build-on top of this, written in lisp (emacs lisp).

I don't really know how you would go about writing something similar with C# - but that's not to say it couldn't be done.

### Post by mode7 on 2014-07-30
Guess it depends..
C++ - It's really been changing a lot for me.. generally I use QT Creator because it can parse CMakeLists, but usually just as a text editor. I make/debug/etc in a terminal on the other monitor.
On Windows, usually Visual Studio. And XCode on OSX, although I rarely use it (Mostly just to see if I can compile something under OSX real quick).

HTML/CSS/JavaScript - Mostly have only done this for school, but I've really grown to like Adobe Brackets.. Simple interface, runs under all major platforms.

C#/.Net - *Really* like MonoDevelop, although now I don't know what's going to happen to it with Xamarin and all, seeing as they don't seem to support Linux at this point. (Have seen PPAs of compiled modern versions of Monodevelop/XamarinStudio, just haven't really tried them)
